When Daniel woke up days later, everything felt different. The steady rhythm of his new heartbeat filled the room like music. He couldn't explain it, but he felt peaceful, almost connected to something beyond himself. The doctor told him the heart came from a young donor who had passed away recently. The family wished to remain anonymous, but they were comforted knowing their loved one's heart had saved a life. Daniel closed his eyes and whispered, "Thank you, whoever you are." His recovery was slow but steady. Each day he grew stronger, walking a little further down the hospital hallway, feeling life return to him. His wife and son visited often, bringing him laughter and warmth. But sometimes late at night, he would feel a strange pull inside his chest, like the heart beating within him carried a story he hadn't heard yet. Months later, he was discharged and allowed to go home. The sun felt brighter, the air fresher. Everything seemed new again. But his perspective on life had changed completely. He no longer thought about schedules or destinations. He thought about moments, the laughter at breakfast, the sound of his son's footsteps, the way his wife smiled when she saw him walk without help. One afternoon, while organizing his things, he found the toy airplane again. He held it in his hand, staring at it with a quiet smile. That little girl's gift had stayed with him through everything. He didn't know where she was now, but he hoped she was happy and healthy. As he placed the toy back on the shelf, a strange feeling passed through him, like warmth spreading through his chest, as if the heart inside him recognized something familiar. He didn't know it then, but the same little girl who once handed him that toy was the very person who had given him the second chance to live. Her heart was now beating inside him, keeping his promise of protection alive in a way neither of them could have ever imagined. The miracle had already happened. It just hadn't revealed itself yet. Life after the transplant felt like waking up from a long, heavy dream. The world looked brighter. Sounds felt clearer. And every heartbeat reminded Daniel that he had been given another chance. It wasn't easy at first. The pain from the surgery, the constant checkups, and the endless pills he had to take. But he didn't complain. He was alive, and that was enough for him. His wife watched over him like a guardian, making sure he ate well, rested, and followed every instruction. Their son would sit by his bedside, showing him drawings of airplanes, proudly saying, "When you get better, we'll fly together, okay?" Daniel would smile, touch his chest lightly, and reply, "Yes, we will." One day soon, weeks turned into months. His strength returned slowly. The doctors were amazed at how well his body accepted the new heart. "You're healing fast," they told him. But what they didn't know was that Daniel often felt something deeper, a strange connection inside him. It wasn't just a physical change. It was as if this heart carried a warmth of its own, a quiet energy that comforted him during lonely nights. Sometimes he would dream. In those dreams, he saw a little girl standing under a bright sky holding a small toy airplane. She never spoke, but she smiled at him. The same innocent smile that had once lit up a cabin full of fear. He always woke up with tears in his eyes, unable to explain why the image felt so real. One morning, Daniel decided to visit the hospital chapel. It was small and quiet with sunlight streaming through the glass windows. He sat there for a long time, his hands folded. Whoever gave me this heart, he whispered. Thank you. I don't know who you are, but I'll live the rest of my life honoring your gift." His voice trembled, but his heart felt light. He didn't know that destiny was already planning to answer his words. Months later, Daniel was invited to a ceremony honoring organ donors and recipients. The hospital wanted to celebrate people who had given or received the gift of life. "His doctor encouraged him to attend." "It might give you peace," he said. Daniel hesitated at first. He wasn't comfortable being the center of attention, but his wife insisted, "You should go. Maybe you'll meet the people who made this miracle possible." The day of the ceremony was calm and sunny. Families filled the hall, holding photos of their loved ones who had donated organs. There were speeches, soft music, and stories that made everyone tear up. Daniel sat quietly in the back, listening. Every word reminded him of how fragile and beautiful life was. When it was his turn to speak, he walked slowly to the stage, his voice steady but emotional. "I don't know who my donor was," he said softly. "But I know their heart beats inside me every day. Whoever they were, they've given me more than life. They've given me purpose. I want to live in a way that honors them. Every breath I take is a reminder of someone's kindness, someone's love." The room fell silent. Many wiped away tears. He finished by saying, "We're all connected, sometimes in ways we can't even see." After the ceremony, as people mingled and shared stories, Daniel stood by a window, looking out at the setting sun. That's when he heard a gentle voice behind him say, "Captain." He turned around slowly and saw a woman standing there. She looked nervous, but kind. There was something familiar about her eyes, though he couldn't place it at first. "You don't know me," she said. "But I think you knew my daughter." For a moment, time stood still. Daniel felt his chest tighten. "Your daughter?" he asked softly. The woman nodded, tears already forming in her eyes. "Her name was Mia. She was on your flight that day." "The one you saved." Daniel's heart stopped for a second. He stared at her speechless. "Mia," he whispered. The woman continued, her voice shaking. "She was very sick. We tried everything, but her little heart couldn't keep up. She passed away a few months ago. She paused, taking a deep breath before adding. Her heart went to a man who needed it to live. We never knew who that person was. Until I saw your name on the ceremony list. Daniel's eyes filled instantly. His hands trembled as he looked at her. "Her heart?" he murmured, unable to finish. The woman nodded gently. "Yes, you have her heart." Tears streamed down both their faces as silence fell between them. Neither could find words big enough for that moment. Finally, the woman said quietly, "She always believed in you. You know, after the flight, she talked about you everyday. She told everyone the pilot kept us safe. He's brave. I want to be like him." She even drew pictures of airplanes and said she'd fly one day. Her voice broke as she smiled sadly. "I think in some way she did." Daniel couldn't hold back anymore. He stepped forward and gently took her hands. I don't know what to say," he whispered. "There are no words for something like this." The woman smiled through her tears. "You don't have to say anything. Just live. That's all she would have wanted." They stood there quietly, the sound of soft music echoing in the background. Daniel felt his heartbeat, her heartbeat strong and steady inside him. It was as if she was still there, reminding him that miracles didn't just happen in the sky. They happened on the ground, too. in the hearts of people who cared for each other. Before leaving, the woman handed him an envelope. She wrote this before before she passed. "I think you should have it." Daniel took it with shaking hands. Later that night at home, he sat by the window and opened it slowly. Inside was a small note written in uneven handwriting. "Thank you for saving us, Captain. You're my hero. Don't be sad. Keep flying. Even if it's not in the sky, I'll always be with you. At the bottom was a small drawing. A pilot and a little girl standing beside an airplane, both smiling. Tears ran down Daniel's face as he held the letter close to his chest. He didn't know how a child could have so much courage, so much love. He whispered her name softly. "Mia," feeling the rhythm of her heart inside him. That moment changed him forever. From that day onward, Daniel lived differently. He started volunteering at hospitals, speaking to families waiting for transplants. He visited schools telling children about kindness and courage. He often said sometimes the smallest heart can make the biggest difference. People listened to him not because he was a pilot or a hero, but because he spoke with a sincerity that came from a place of real experience. Every morning he would stand outside and look up at the sky, feeling the wind on his face. He no longer missed flying because he realized he was already part of something much bigger than any airplane could carry. He had been given a second chance to live and to love, to give, to inspire. And somewhere deep inside him, he knew that little girl was still with him. Every heartbeat, every smile, every act of kindness carried her spirit forward. It wasn't just his heart anymore. It was theirs, beating together, reminding the world that sometimes miracles are not seen. They are felt. Time passed, but the story didn't fade. Daniel's life became a quiet example of how one act of kindness could ripple through the lives of many. He no longer wore his pilot's uniform, but everyone still called him captain. To the world, he was the man who had survived against all odds. To his family, he was a husband and father who had been given back to them. But deep inside, Daniel carried something even greater, a promise to live for two hearts, not one. He often visited the park where children played, watching them laugh and run freely. Sometimes when he saw a little girl holding a toy airplane, his chest would ache, not in pain, but in remembrance. It felt like Mia was still there somewhere, smiling from a distance. He had kept her letter safely framed in his study along with the drawing she made. Whenever he looked at it, he felt a calm strength as if her spirit was guiding him. One afternoon, Daniel was invited by a local school to talk about courage and hope. At first, he didn't want to. He wasn't sure what to say that would truly matter to children. But his wife encouraged him. "You don't need a perfect speech," she said. "Just tell your story the way it happened. That's enough." So, he agreed. When he arrived at the school, the students gathered in the hall, curious and excited. Daniel stood before them with a gentle smile. "I used to fly airplanes," he began. I loved being in the sky. But one day, I learned that the real flight isn't about airplanes. It's about hearts. The children listened closely, their eyes wide as he shared how life had tested him, how a young girl's courage had saved him. He didn't mention names, only feelings. The fear, the loss, the miracle. When he finished, the room was silent. Then a small boy raised his hand and asked, "Do you still miss the sky?" Daniel smiled softly. "Every day," he said. But now I know that sometimes the best way to fly is to lift others up. The teachers clapped, the students smiled, and for the first time in a long while, Daniel felt peaceful. After that, he began speaking more often. Hospitals, schools, community events, wherever people needed hope, he went. He didn't ask for anything in return. He simply shared his journey, reminding everyone that life was fragile, but also full of grace. Slowly, his story began spreading. Newspapers wrote about him and people sent letters thanking him for giving them strength. One letter stood out. It came from a father who wrote, "Your story helped my son agree to a heart transplant he was afraid of." He said, "If the pilot can do it, I can, too. You saved him." Daniel read that letter over and over, tears filling his eyes. It made him realize that maybe this was his new calling, not flying planes, but giving people hope. Years went by. Daniel's health stayed stable, though he never forgot to take his medicines or attend his checkups. He treated everyday as a gift. On anniversaries, he would visit the donor wall at the hospital. He'd bring a small bunch of white flowers and stand quietly, eyes closed, whispering a silent thank you to Mia and her family. Her mother still kept in touch, and their bond had grown deeper over time. They didn't talk about loss anymore. They talked about life, about how her daughter's heart had not just kept beating, but had kept inspiring. One winter evening, Daniel received a call from a journalist making a short documentary about organ donation. They wanted him to share his experience on film. At first, he hesitated. He had always been humble about his story, never seeking attention. But then he thought of all the people who might find courage in hearing it. So, he agreed. The filming took place in his home. The crew asked gentle questions while he sat near a window, the evening light falling softly across his face. He spoke with warmth and honesty. When they asked him what he had learned from everything he'd been through, he paused for a moment before answering. I've learned that life isn't measured by how long we live, but by how much love we give while we're here. I was given another chance not just to live but to make a difference and that's what I want to keep doing as long as this heart beats. The documentary was short but powerful. When it was released, thousands of people watched it online. Many shared their own stories of receiving or donating organs. Families wrote messages saying they had found hope again. Daniel didn't see himself as a hero, but he felt grateful that Mia's story had continued to touch lives through him. One afternoon while taking a walk near the lake, Daniel saw a little girl sitting with her mother folding paper airplanes. The girl looked up and smiled at him holding one out. "Do you want to try?" she asked. He laughed and sat beside her. Together, they folded another paper plane. When they threw it into the wind, it flew high, spinning and gliding gracefully before landing softly on the grass. The girl clapped and said, "You're good at this." Daniel smiled. I've had a bit of practice," he said gently. As he watched the plane drift away, he thought about how life was like that, fragile, unpredictable, but still beautiful if you let it soar. The wind carried the paper plane higher, and for a moment, he felt as though Mia was watching, too. That evening, he returned home and opened his journal. He wrote, "Today, I threw a paper airplane. It reminded me that no matter how far we go, a part of us always stays with the ones we love. Maybe that's what flying really means. Not leaving, but carrying hearts together. His wife read those words later and hugged him. You've changed, you know, she said softly. You used to chase the sky. Now the sky seems to chase you. Daniel smiled. Maybe it's just her way of saying hello. As years rolled on, Daniel grew older, his hair turning gray, his steps slower. But his spirit stayed young. He continued to visit schools and hospitals, always telling his story in the same simple, heartfelt way. People never got tired of hearing it. They said it reminded them that even in pain, there could be beauty, and even in loss, there could be life. One day, as he stood in front of a group of medical students, he said something that stayed with everyone who heard it. I used to believe that a pilot's job was to guide a plane through storms. But now I know that our real job in life is to guide each other through the storms we face. That's what keeps the world flying. After the talk, a young student came up to him with tears in her eyes. "My brother needs a transplant," she said quietly. "I was scared before, but hearing you gave me hope." "Thank you." Daniel placed a gentle hand on her shoulder and said, "Hope is like an airplane. It can't fly without someone believing it will." That night, as Daniel lay in bed, he placed a hand over his heart. He could still feel it beating strong and steady, the rhythm of two lives intertwined. He closed his eyes, and for a brief moment, he saw the same dream again. A bright sky, a little girl, and a paper airplane soaring high. This time, she waved, and he waved back, smiling peacefully. He knew then that everything had come full circle. He had kept his promise. He had lived for her, for himself, for everyone who had ever needed a reason to believe again. And as the stars shone quietly above, Daniel felt something deep and comforting. The feeling that even when his journey ended someday, his story, like that paper airplane, would keep flying forever.

It is an entirely&nbsp;&nbsp; different matter for the people flying the plane&nbsp; as there are still plenty of things to do even&nbsp;&nbsp; after takeoff. Let’s look at what’s happening&nbsp; on the flight deck during these longer flights… Even though flight paths are decided upon&nbsp; before departure, pilots have to monitor&nbsp;&nbsp; weather conditions and decide whether&nbsp; or not to alter them during the flight.&nbsp;&nbsp; On long-haul flights, weather conditions are&nbsp; more likely to change from initial forecasts,&nbsp;&nbsp; with aircraft often encountering multiple&nbsp; weather systems. These systems can vary in&nbsp;&nbsp; intensity from slight turbulence to dangerous&nbsp; thunderstorms. Traveling at high speeds,&nbsp;&nbsp; pilots must be prepared at all times&nbsp; to alter the flight path if necessary. If, for example, they see a&nbsp; thunderstorm on the radar,&nbsp;&nbsp; they must first get the OK from air traffic&nbsp; control (ATC) to change their route. Clear air turbulence is another worry, and&nbsp; because it cannot be seen on the radar,&nbsp;&nbsp; pilots rely upon the information provided by&nbsp; pilots flying the same route ahead of them. When&nbsp;&nbsp; flying across oceans such as the Atlantic, one&nbsp; pilot's job is to communicate with the ATC while&nbsp;&nbsp; the other monitors a common air-to-air frequency&nbsp; that pilots use to communicate with each other. The entire time a flight is&nbsp; in the air, the pilots monitor&nbsp;&nbsp; all kinds of gauges to ensure that the&nbsp; plane performs how it is supposed to. Making sure that the temperature of the fuel&nbsp; is not too cold is another thing pilots must&nbsp;&nbsp; monitor. If the fuel cools down too much,&nbsp; its flow can be restricted due to waxing.&nbsp;&nbsp; If this happens, pilots can rectify&nbsp; the situation by descending, or by&nbsp;&nbsp; increasing the aircraft's speed (and,&nbsp; thus, its skin temperature). However,&nbsp;&nbsp; this isn't a particularly frequent occurrence,&nbsp; as engine oil helps fuel to retain heat. While one of the pilots monitors all the&nbsp; gauges, the other will be busy taking care&nbsp;&nbsp; of the paperwork. Before the flight, the&nbsp; pilots are given a written flight plan.&nbsp;&nbsp; Anything that deviates from this&nbsp; must be written down as notes. Outside of these important activities,&nbsp; long-haul flying can be very boring for pilots.&nbsp;&nbsp; During cruise phases, pilots will often pass&nbsp; the time by making small talk with one another,&nbsp;&nbsp; or doing some reading. Smartphone games and&nbsp; good old fashioned crossword puzzles might be&nbsp;&nbsp; additional forms of entertainment&nbsp; found on the flight deck. Finally, just like passengers,&nbsp; enjoying inflight meals&nbsp;&nbsp; is another part of the long-haul pilot experience. The pilots typically wait until the passengers&nbsp; have been served and then eat separately.&nbsp;&nbsp; They do this so that there is&nbsp; always one person at the controls. For long flights over twelve hours, there&nbsp; are one or two relief pilots onboard&nbsp;&nbsp; so that the two pilots who performed the&nbsp; takeoff can take a break and get some rest. There are just two pilots on shorter&nbsp; long-haul flights of eight or nine&nbsp;&nbsp; hours. The only opportunity they get to&nbsp; leave their seats and stretch their legs&nbsp;&nbsp; is when they go to the bathroom. Did you know about these long-haul duties and&nbsp; activities? - No FD Takeoff Procedure | Airbus A320 Pilot: Kind: captions Language: en In this video, we will discuss no FDs takeoff. Normally, pilots are trained to perform takeoff with FDS on. This is standard day-to-day airline operation. It is important to understand why FDS are used and what to do when they are not available. FDS provide clear visual guidance to the pilot. They give pitch and bank commands on the primary flight display. During takeoff, workload is high. Pilots must monitor speed, thrust, runway tracking, and aircraft altitude. FDS reduce this workload. They remove the need for mental calculation, and crew simply follows the command bars. This helps achieve the correct takeoff speed and the correct initial climb path. Since takeoff is a critical phase of flight, the focus should be on procedure execution, not on thinking or calculating. Wondering why no FD's takeoff is still important? Over time, pilots become dependent on flight directors. With frequent use, the basic technique of flying without FD guidance can be forgotten. However, no FD's takeoff may still be required. This can be due to male requirements, system failures, or procedural constraints. In such cases, the pilot must fly the aircraft in basic modes. This video will help you quickly recall the NOFD's takeoff technique if such a situation arises. Let first focus on what changes during a NOFD's takeoff. During a NOFD's takeoff, there is no vertical or lateral guidance. The pilot must manually manage pitch, speed, bank angle, navigation, and thrust. Auto thrust guidance linked to SRS is also not available. The aircraft must be flown manually using basic pitch and power principles. This requires higher alertness and discipline. Plan no FDs takeoff. For a plan no FDs takeoff, there is a simple way to remember the procedure. The pneummonic is SAF. S for speed. Select climb speed. The default speed is 100 knots. D for thrust. Set thrust levers to climb to tent. A for auto thrust. Activate auto thrust. F for flight directors. Turn on the flight directors. Once selected on, FDs will operate in basic modes such as heading and vertical speed. This completes the no FD's takeoff procedure. There is another scenario to consider. If both flight directors fail after takeoff has commenced, the FD bars disappear during the takeoff run. The FCU speed window will display a target speed. This speed will be V2 or current speed, whichever is higher. Auto thrust remains armed. At thrust reduction altitude, lever climb will flash. The pilot must set thrust levers to climb again. Apply the STAF procedure. Select speed. Set thrust. Activate auto thrust. Turn on flight directors. At acceleration altitude, the target speed will not automatically increase. The pilot must manually accelerate to green dot or clean configuration speed. Another important case is takeoff with no V2 entered in the FMGC. If V2 is not inserted, SRS will not engage. 5 seconds after liftoff, vertical speed mode engages and the current speed becomes the FCU target speed. In this case, the aircraft must be flown manually. Speed must be selected manually at acceleration altitude and an appropriate vertical mode must be selected such as vertical speed or open climb. Lateral mode will depend on FMGC or FCU selection. Managed NAV or selected heading. Remember this clearly. SRS is linked to V2. No V2 entry means no SRS guidance. Flight directors simplify takeoff but pilots must remain capable of flying without them. No FD takeoff is about discipline, basic flying skills, correct use of procedures. Understanding these scenarios ensures safe aircraft handling when automation is unavailable. If you liked the video, please don't forget to like, share, and subscribe to our

Unidentified aircraft. This is Falcon 1 of the US Air Force. You are entering restricted airspace. Identify yourself immediately. Ben froze. Aaron leaned forward, pressed the transmit button, and in a tone colder than steel said, "This is Ghost Rider requesting vector confirmation." There was silence, absolute silence on all frequencies. Then another voice came through lower, filled with disbelief. Say again, call sign. Aaron exhaled slowly. Ghost rider. Copy. The cockpit went dead quiet. Even the hum of the engines seemed to fade across the secure military channels. Disbelief rippled like thunder. The legend the pilot who had disappeared was now speaking from a commercial cockpit. and the men in the raptors, they had grown up hearing stories about him. One of them, Captain Miles Trent, had a photograph in his locker of Ghost Rider standing beside an F-15 back when he was a child watching his father serve under that same man. Now he was intercepting him in the air. Aaron didn't know it yet, but he had just changed the course of his life again. down below. The restricted airspace issue was being handled. A software glitch had misouted his civilian flight path. But in that short span of confusion, a dozen military control rooms came alive. They traced the voice print, verified it twice. It's him. One technician whispered, "It's ghost rider." That evening, after landing safely, Aaron walked through the terminal like any other pilot. He smiled at passengers, thanked the crew, and went home to Isa. But as he tucked her in, his phone buzzed with a restricted number. He didn't want to answer, but something told him to. Aaron Whitlock. The voice said, "This is Colonel James Cutter, US Air Force Command. We need to talk." Aaron's heart sank. That name doesn't exist anymore, he said quietly. The colonel's voice softened. Maybe, but Ghost Rider just saved 142 lives up there today. We'd like to know how. It turned out that while Aaron's flight computer had glitched, his quick manual recalibration done from muscle memory had prevented a catastrophic descent. The data showed he performed a maneuver that wasn't even taught in commercial aviation. It was something only a few Air Force pilots knew how to execute. Cutter wanted him back not as a fighter pilot, but as a mentor for a new program training young pilots to handle crisis situations in civil aviation. Aaron hesitated. Returning meant facing the ghosts of his past, the ones he had buried with his Air Force uniform. But when Isa found him staring at the old photo of his squadron that night, she said softly, "Daddy, were you a hero once?" He looked at her for a long time before whispering, "I tried to be." The next morning he drove to Nellie's Air Force base, the desert wind tugging at the edges of his resolve. The hangers stood like sleeping giants, and when he walked in, silence fell. Men who had only heard stories about Ghost Rider stood up straighter. Cutter met him halfway, offering a firm handshake. "Welcome back, sir," he said. Over the next months, Aaron trained pilots not in combat, but in control, teaching them that precision and calm could save lives in any sky. Among them was Captain Miles Trent, the young F22 pilot who had intercepted him. The two developed an unspoken bond respect born of shared skies and quiet admiration. But life as always had one more test to give. 6 months later, a freak weather system crippled the power grid in the western states. Communication towers went down, radar systems flickered, and chaos spread through the skies. Aaron was aboard a small private training jet with a new recruit guiding him through instrument failure drills when the real storm hit. Lightning cracked through the clouds and their systems went blind. Ground control couldn't reach them. The recruit panicked, shouting through static, but Aaron stayed calm, his voice steady. Fly by feel. Trust your instincts, he said. Meanwhile, in the control tower, Miles recognized the last radio ping. "It's him," he whispered. "Ghost riders in the storm. He and two other pilots scrambled into their F-22s, slicing through the chaos to reach him. For 2 hours, the storm raged. The world below didn't know that one man was out there keeping his student alive while risking his own life." When the fuel warning light blinked red, Aaron made a choice. He guided the rookie toward an airirstrip's faint lights and stayed behind, circling just long enough to ensure his student made it down safely. The last radar contact showed a blip disappearing behind thunderclouds. Rescue teams found the wreckage miles away. The plane was destroyed, but somehow, impossibly, Aaron was alive, battered, bleeding, but alive. When asked later why he stayed up there, he said, "Because someone once stayed up there for me." That night, as he lay in the hospital, Isa climbed onto his bed, clutching his hand. "You are a hero, Daddy," she whispered. And for the first time in years, Aaron believed it. The Air Force reinstated his service record with honor, marking his name, not under combat achievements, but under service to humanity. He never returned to combat again. He didn't need to. His new mission was bigger than war. It was hope. So when people hear about the day F-22 pilots heard the name Ghost Rider on a civilian frequency, they remember it not as a mystery, but as a story of redemption, a reminder that some legends don't die, they just change their wings. If this story touched your heart, please like, share, and subscribe to Kindness Journal. Your support helps us share more real stories of courage, love, and second chances. And before we end, take a moment to comment below. What does redemption mean to you? Because somewhere out there, another ghost rider is waiting for a reason to believe

- Auto-pilot Parenting?: Kind: captions Language: en Hi, it's Andrew from Rite Journey Parenting Plan here and I just wanted to chat with you today a little bit about the bigger picture of the Rite Journey Parenting Plan and it sort of comes in the title the fact that I think it's really important that we actually have a plan on how we parent and so, I've been thinking about this a little bit and sort of imagining that in most journeys that we take, we actually have a picture of the destination where we're actually heading which then enables us to take the correct path to that particular destination. I think with parenting though probably, we don't do that terribly well. So, what I'm suggesting is that it's really important that we do get a picture of what kind of young adult we'd like to be creating in our parenting and it did get me into thinking, if you don't actually have a destination, then it can be quite difficult to know whether you're on track to that destination and what came to mind for me was the picture of an autopilot and the fact that when an autopilot in a plane is set up, obviously they know where their destination is and the way the autopilot actually works is that when it gets off track, it corrects itself to get back on track, so it's not always flying the exact direction, the exact course to its destination. There are outside influences that come along that actually push it a bit off track but what it does know is it knows how to get back onto track, so the autopilot actually kicks in and swings it back around towards its destination. So, what is our destination as a parent? What are we hoping to create? And I think that is a really important conversation that we need to have, we need to think about it ourselves, if we have a co-parent, we need to talk about it with them but only then can you actually think about what the path is going to be to getting to that destination. So, some things I think are important to think about and what kind of qualities you'd like your children to hold. What are the virtues that would be important for them to have as adults? What words would you hope come to mind when thinking about your child when they become an adult? Certainly in my ponderings about the Rite Journey, the words that have come up and I've used for many years now, we want them to be resilient, we'd like them to be responsible, respectful, resourceful, we talk about shifting from dependence into independence and then there'll be a whole lot of other words which will come to mind that you hope are part of your young adult's life like love. And so, the parenting plan and your family culture really needs to be built around that particular destination. I don't think there would be many businesses that would start without some kind of mission statement, without some kind of purpose, some kind of direction and yet often we launch into our parenting without really having that clearly in mind. And so, that's really what the Rite Journey Parenting Plan is partly about is working out what the direction is that you want to be going with your children, what the destination point is and then creating some kind of path, putting some things in place, creating a family culture that will help you reach that destination and then if you get a little bit off track, hopefully there's a co-parent or someone else in your life who's able to step in, maybe it's even you yourself noticing that you're off track and just guide yourself back around so that you can keep moving as best on track as you can with that destination in mind. All of your support helps us make psychology and mental health more accessible to everyone. So thank you for all of the love and support. Now, back to the video. How often do you feel lonely? While it's common for all of us to experience loneliness every now and then, what do we do with the feeling persists more than we're used to? If not dealt with properly, loneliness can grow into something harmful to a mental health. Now, if you believe you're suffering from excessive or chronic loneliness, a good plan might be to seek out help through a counselor or mental health professional. It's a great first step and gives you a chance to talk with someone about your feelings that you're experiencing. While a lot of us can feel lonely when isolated for too long, loneliness isn't reliant on being alone. Loneliness, like all of our emotions, is a state of mind. Research demonstrates that loneliness does not reflect how many friends or family members you have by your side. Instead, it's how connected you feel internally. You can be surrounded by family or friends, and yet your feelings of loneliness still somehow managed to persist. So what can we do about it? Well, you're in luck, because here at Psych2go we've gathered a list of four tips to help you deal with loneliness. Number one, become friends with yourself first. Self care is something that is extremely important for our mental health. The truth is, we may feel lonely because deep down we longed for our own friendship. When's the last time you were kind to yourself, took a minute to relax and wind down just for you? And I don't mean passing out after a long night of working. I mean conscious time dedicated to yourself and your thoughts, thoughts all on you, not work, not drama, just relaxation. This could mean a relaxing bubble bath instead of a quick shower. Some time spent doing an activity or a hobby that you enjoy, or perhaps sitting down with your favorite book. When loneliness comes knocking on the door, it's a good idea to look inwards and spend some time with the friend within ourselves. They may be the one to help us calm ourselves and relieve some stress. That is if we do spend time with them. Once we've made friends with ourselves, we might not feel as lonely around others or on our own, because you always know you got your back. Number two, show compassion. Oftentimes, when we're lonely, we may wish that someone could simply be there for us, someone to understand us and show us some compassion. Well, a little compassion can go a long way. Don't you feel a little cheerier when someone gives you a compliment? when someone does a favor for you? Don't you feel a little more valued and less alone? Wouldn't you wanna bring that compassion to others as well and spread love? Because not only does carrying out kind acts for others help them feel cheery, but it can make us feel cheery as well, and less lonely in the process. After all, we're engaging in a form of social interaction. Compassion can be a step towards making a new friend, or simply feeling less lonely knowing you may have made someone's day. According to research from psychologists, Ed Diener and Martin Seligman, altruism can lead to improved mental and physical health as well as speed up recovery from diseases. Brain imaging research from Naomi Eisenberger at UCLA has found that providing support for others, may have unique positive effects on key brain areas involved in stress and reward responses. The research shows that a little compassion and support can predict decreased stress responses in the brain. So next time you're feeling lonely, perhaps reach out to a family member or old friend who could use some support, donate to a charity or volunteer with an organization you care about, maybe a mental health organization on loneliness, I'm sure they'd love to support as much as you. Number three, find your tribe. As mentioned, you don't need to be physically alone to feel lonely. You could be at a loud, seemingly fun party with others and still you can't help but shake the feeling. Perhaps you do have friends or classmates you regularly talk to and you do get along with them, but you don't necessarily have a lot in common with them. You may even find yourself disinterested in the topics they discuss, but politely not alone because hey they're your friends after all, and you care about them. But sometimes you feel like the odd one out. This may be due to the idea that you haven't found your tribe yet. Tribe, what on earth do you mean? Well, looking for your tribe means you're looking for people who share common interests and ideas with you or possess traits you aspire to adapt. We should all surround ourselves with others who share the same interest, so we can not only discuss what we value, but gain a friend in doing so. It can take some people many years to find their tribe, they don't appear out of thin air. That's why joining an online community with interests similar to your own is an easy place to start. If you enjoy a certain video game, reach out to online players and make some friends. Or perhaps you're a book lover, join a book club online or in person. You may find yourself with your very own tribe in no time. Number four, there is no perfect trend. If you're lonely, you may have daydreamed about what it would be like to be perfectly socialized, imagining yourself with an ideal group of friends and your best friend, the perfect friend. Hate to burst your bubble, but there's no such thing as perfect, meaning your imaginary friend isn't all you'd hoped they'd be. Theory and research suggests that those with a high level of perfectionism are more at risk for depressive symptoms and suicide ideation, because their feelings of alienation, isolation and loneliness are all the more intense. Thing is, perfectionism and socializing may even prevent them from socializing at all. Because when they do look for the perfect friend, they can't seem to find them anywhere. Philosopher and author of "A Philosophy of Loneliness" Lars Svendsen, argues that people with chronic loneliness may likely be social perfectionist. Svendsen explains that social perfectionism is more common among lonely individuals than non lonely. The lonely person thinks that they're unloved and that no one will befriend them. But perhaps the problem is rather that because they place such impossible demands on friendship and love, they are not capable of loving or befriending someone. Now, are you capable of befriending someone? Absolutely. Don't let a little perfectionism get in the way of making a new friend. People have flaws, and not every social interaction is meant to be amazing. It can be awkward at times, or even silly. Once we're content with knowing friendships can be messy, awkward and silly at times, we can then feel a little more relaxed when it comes to making a new friend. If you think your perfectionism is getting in the way of finding a friend, know that someone is out there for you, but they won't be perfect. And flaws and all, you'll still love each other and may even be a little less lonely. There's someone out there for you, you just have to keep looking. So, will you try out any of these tips? What online communities will you join? Let us know in the comments. The studies and references used are listed in the description below. Don't forget to hit the subscribe button and notification by icon for more Psych2go videos and thank you for watching. And if this video helped you, please let us know by liking and sharing it with someone who may be feeling lonely. After all, this video could be a great way to reach out and support someone who is suffering from loneliness. And in the process, you never know, you may just make a new friend.

  18. @tiataro8700: I think what people don’t talk about with loneliness is how confusing it can be.

I’ve had people tell me I’m safe to be around, easy to talk to, someone they gravitate toward… and yet I still find myself alone sometimes.

It’s not that I can’t connect. It’s that a lot of connections feel conditional or surface-level, and I don’t really fit into that. I don’t want friendships that only exist when it’s convenient or when everything looks good from the outside.

So you end up in this strange in-between space where you know you’re capable of deep connection, you feel things deeply, you show up for people… but you haven’t quite found the people who meet you there consistently.

And that can feel like something is wrong with you, even when it’s not.

Maybe some of us aren’t “alone” because we’re unlikable.

Maybe we’re just still looking for something real.

This guys face seemed mesmerizing to him, like the highest form of beauty. Only later did he realize that what this handsome guy REALLY seemed to symbolize was a very relaxed face and a very relaxed personality. Maybe he was self-reliant and safe and just expressed a very relaxed face. My gay friend on the other hand always had strived to be good enough, happy enough, fun enough et cetera. He had forgotten to reach out for himself. He was a slave and now he loved his master. He kind of lost it all somewhere. So being relaxed was the real beauty cue in this straight guy’s face.  So forget idealizing beauty and social media-fuckups. It’s ok to think someone is beautiful – don’t deny that feeling – but strive for your own goals. All these things you can achieve also without an obejctively "beautiful face" or "hypersexy body", and actually maybe even to a larger degree than them. A model that might be false or simplified. It might be some superficial therapy model or in worst case a try to envite you into the psychologists own self-deceptive imagination. Instead, save your money and go to the library. Borrow the world litterature, the nobel prize laureates, like Albert Camus, J P Sartre, Hemingway, Doris Lessing et cetera. And you will find a new friends in their voices. There is a good reason these people won the Nobel prize in litterature. They had the courage to see the world the way it is, and to share this view in magical letters. The path toward feeling better about yourself, including opening up to the world around, goes through an unwavering belief in REALITY, and not the tv-shows, fashion magazines or Facebook, and also if it might at first seem becoming more cynical or lose your nice illusions. You go outside instead, and say hello to a squirrel! 😊
