- Your Kid Wants to Be a Pilot? Here’s What Parents Need to Know: Kind: captions Language: en If you're watching this, there's a good chance your child just came up to you and said, "Mom or dad, I want to be a pilot." So, what now? Maybe you're excited and proud. Maybe you're a bit nervous or just completely in the dark about what that actually means. I get it. When I told my own parents I wanted to fly, they looked at me like I said I wanted to be an astronaut. They had no idea what the journey entailed. Fast forward to today. I'm a certified flight instructor and soon to be airline pilot, and I've made it my mission to help demystify the process for families like yours. In this video, I'm going to walk you through everything a parent needs to know when their kid wants to become a pilot. We'll break down what flight training actually involves, how long it takes, the timeline from first lesson to professional career, how much it costs, and yes, we'll be real about the costs, important safety info, because I know you're probably worried, the different career paths in aviation, and how you can best support your child's dreams. We'll also tackle some common myths like, do pilots need perfect vision? Do you have to be a math genius? Is it insanely dangerous? And point out some red flags to watch for when choosing a flight school or flight program. Stick around because by the end of this video, you'll have a clear road map and hopefully some peace of mind about this whole piloting thing. So whether you're totally on board with the idea or feeling a bit anxious, let's dive in together. This is definitely going to be a much longer video than I've been making lately. So sit down, get comfy, grab a piece of paper, grab a pencil, take some notes, and let's get through it together. Your kid wants to be a pilot. Let's help them get there safely and smartly. Just a complete side note, I actually got this video idea from my mom herself. So, shout out to you, Mom. Let's start with the basics. What does flight training actually look like? A lot of people think learning to fly is just hopping in a plane and taking off, but there's a lot more to it. Flight training is a structured process that includes both ground school, which is classroom or online learning, and flight lessons in the air. On the ground, your kid will study the fundamentals of aviation, how airplanes work, the rules of the sky, airspace and regulations, basic aerodynamics, navigation, meteorology, and safety procedures. They'll need to pass a written knowledge exam to show they understand that material. It's a bit like the written test for a driver's license, but way more in depth. Don't worry, there are courses and books and lots and lots of practice tests to help with this to make sure that they're well prepared. In the air, they'll be training in a small airplane, often a two or four-seater like a Cessna 172 with a certified flight instructor or CFI sitting next to them. The instructor will teach them how to handle the aircraft step by step. Early on, the instructor does most of the flying to demonstrate how to take off, how to turn, climb, descend, and of course, how to land the airplane. Quickly though, the student begins to do those things under guidance. They'll learn basic maneuvers, flying straight and level, turns, altitude changes, and how to recover from mistakes, how to navigate to other airports, and what to do in emergencies, like if the engine quits. Yes, they practice that safely. It's very hands-on. A huge early milestone in flight training is the first solo flight. That's the first time your child will fly the airplane alone without the instructor on board. Now, before you panic, know that the instructor will not let them solo until they are truly ready and have demonstrated the necessary skills and judgment repeatedly. There are specific requirements a student must meet before solo. They have to be at least 16 years old. They have to have a certain level of proficiency. And they also have to have a medical and the instructor's endorsement saying, "I trust them to do this." The first solo typically happens somewhere in the 15 to 20 hours of instruction. It varies a lot though. Some do it earlier, some do it later. It's all based on ability and comfort level. It usually consists of a couple takeoffs and landings in the traffic pattern while the instructor watches from the ground, probably more nervous than the student. It's an incredible confidence booster. I still remember mine vividly and I'm pretty sure my smile was visible from space that day. After solo, the training continues with more advanced stuff. Flying to other airports, which we call cross-country flight, flying at night and polishing all those skills. Eventually, when the instructor feels that the student is fully prepared, they'll go for their check ride. A check ride is like the final exam. It's like a driving test for the pilot's license. An FA designated examiner will spend a couple hours asking oral questions and then go up in the plane with the student to verify that they can perform all of the required maneuvers and handle scenarios safely. If all goes well, they'll land and the examiner will say, "Congratulations, you're now a pilot." And they earn their private pilot certificate. This is the first big license that allows them to fly small aircraft and even carry passengers, friends, or family for non-commercial purposes. So, let's summarize it. Flight training is a mix between ground school and flight lessons, leading to milestones like the first solo and then a flight test to get a pilot's license. It's very much like learning to drive, but in three dimensions and with more theory to learn. It's structured, but it's also a lot of fun. Many students describe their lessons as the highlight of their week. It's challenging, but in a good, rewarding way. And as a parent, you'll get to share in the excitement of each achievement. from that first takeoff they get to do to the day they proudly hold their pilot certificate in their hand. Timeline overview. How long does it take to go from zero experience to actually being a pilot or even an airline pilot? The timeline can vary widely, but I'll give you a general overview of the typical journey. First, let's distinguish goals. If your child just wants to fly for fun recreationally, then they might stop after getting a private pilot's license, which could be achieved in a matter of a few months to a year depending on training frequency. However, since many young people who dream of being a pilot envision it as being a career, I'll outline the full pathway to professional pilot. Private pilot's license timeline 3 to 12 months. The minimum training time for a PPL is 40 flight hours, but most people train somewhere between 60 to 75 or or even more before they're test ready because everyone learns at their own pace and you want to be fully competent. If a student is training full-time, say four to five lessons a week, they might finish in three to four months. If they're doing it part-time, like one lesson a week on weekends or working around school schedules, it could take 6 months, 9 months, a year. It's typically pretty flexible. The key is consistency. Training goes faster if they can fly at least a couple times a week. With long gaps, they spend time relearning skills. So roughly many high schoolers or college students may start in the spring and solo by summer then licensed by fall. Others might take a full year because of other commitments. Both are totally fine. Next is your instrument rating. The timeline for this is about 2 to 4 months if you're training full-time. After getting your private pilot's license, the next big step for a careerbound pilot is the instrument rating. This training teaches them how to fly the airplane with reference to instruments alone, meaning that they can safely navigate if there's low visibility or clouds by trusting their cockpit instruments. It's crucial for anyone who wants to be a professional pilot since obviously airlines fly in all weather. Instrument training requires a minimum of 40 hours of instrument flight time. Often, it's a mix of real flight and simulator time. This could be done in a couple of months if done full-time or over several months part-time. Many combine this with the next step. And the next step is your commercial pilot's license. Typically between 6 to 12 months, including the time building period. The commercial pilot certificate is what allows a pilot to start flying for compensation or higher. For example, getting paid to fly. To earn a commercial license, a pilot needs more experience. At least 250 total flight hours under FAA rules if training under part 61. That means after getting a private license at say roughly 60 hours, they need roughly another 190 hours of flying, which is often the longest phase of training. During this time, they might time build by renting planes to fly cross-country trips or splitting time with friends or doing an instructor-led commercial training syllabus that includes some advanced maneuvers and procedures. Some pilots get their multi-engine rating during this phase as well, so they can log the hours in a twin engine aircraft. The timeline here really depends on how they go about it. If enrolled in an academy program, they might have a structured schedule to reach 250 hours and complete commercial training in say six months after instrument. If doing it more gradually, maybe finishing college while flying on weekends, it could take a year or more. By the end of this stage, they'll have a commercial pilot's license, possibly with single engine and multi-engine privileges, and maybe around 250 to 300 total hours of flight experience. Next is the certified flight instructor in the hour building phase. This typically takes about two years. Many new commercial pilots become instructors. Why? Because to get a job at an airline or most other advanced commercial operations, you typically need 1,500 hours of flight experience. That's the requirement for an airline transport pilot certificate. Going from about 250 hours to 1500 hours means gaining 1,250 more hours. Working as a flight instructor is a common way to do that. and someone actually pays them rather than them paying for flight time. Gaining a CFI certificate involves another training course and a check ride. And there are additional instructor ratings like CFI for instrument instructing and MEI for multi-engine. Once they start instructing, how long it takes to build hours depends on how much they fly. A busy full-time instructor might log about 800 hours per year. It took me about 2 years to get from 250 up to,500 hours by instructing full-time. Some people supplement with other flying gigs like towing banners, flying skydive jumpers, etc. So, you're looking at perhaps two years of instructing or other entry-level jobs to reach the magic number of hours for the airlines airline or other projob hiring. The regional airlines, which operates smaller jets, often feeder flights for major airlines, are usually the first stop once a pilot hits the requirements. Typically, you interview for a company somewhere around the,000hour mark so that you can start very close to getting your 1500 hours. That all drastically depends on how the hiring market is at that specific moment. But let's assume about 1500 hours in age 23. That's the age for an unrestricted airline transport pilot. From there, they'll spend a few months in training with the airline, ground school, and simulator for that specific jet, and then start flying as a first officer or co-pilot. After one to two years, they might upgrade to captain at a regional depending on the airlines needs and their experience. Many then after that jump to a major airline, that's your Delta American United or a large cargo airline after accumulating about maybe 3,000 hours in some jet experience. There's a lot of variability. Some may choose corporate aviation at this juncture instead, and we'll talk about that later. So, if we add that up, a fasttrack scenario might look like start at 18, private by 19, commercial and instructor by 20,500 hours by 22, and airline by 23. That's very brisk and assumes full-time dedication. A more spread out scenario could be start at 18, but go to college while training, maybe reach commercial by 22, instruct part-time through college, hit 1500 hours by 25, airline by mid to late 20s. There's no single right path. I know pilots who didn't start until their 30s or later. They took longer but still reached the airlines eventually. There's no upper age limit to start training. You just need to be 16 to solo and 17 to get a license. Beyond that, your age isn't a barrier to training. The point is, becoming a pilot is a multi-stage journey, and each stage has its own time frame. For you as a parent, it helps you know these stages because you can celebrate each one as a victory. First, they'll be excited to get that first license, their private pilot's license. Then, they'll build on it. It's not an all or nothing proposition. Even if someone doesn't go all the way to airline pilot, each certificate they earn is a useful qualification. And if they do aim for the airlines, yes, it's several years of building experience. But the long-term forecast is that this industry really needs pilots. So, opportunities are out there and they should be out there when your kid reaches that point. There's one more thing I want to talk about on the timeline. There are some flight schools that advertise from going zero to commercial pilot in about 9 months. These can work for some people, but you have to dedicate 100% of your time to it, and it is a very intensive pace, but don't worry if your child's path is slower. It's not a race, and sometimes a bit more maturity and life experience can help along the way. The timeline can also be influenced by external factors. Weather delays, availability of instructors or airplanes, passing exams on the first try, needing extra attempts, etc. Flexibility and perseverance are key. As the saying goes, a pilot's license is a certificate of learning, not a certificate of perfection. Everyone moves at their own pace. Cost breakdown. All right, let's talk about the part parents often care about the most, the cost. There's no way around it. Flight training is a huge financial investment. It's often cited as one of the main reasons aspiring pilots give up, but with proper planning and support, it can be managed. I'll break down typical costs in the US, but costs can differ internationally, but the general proportions probably hold. Keep in mind these are rough ballpark ranges. Actual costs depend on the local price of aircraft rentals, fuel, instructors fees, etc. But this should give you a rough idea. Your private pilot's license is roughly going to be 12 to $18,000 in the US. This includes about 50 to 60 hours of flight time at say about $250 an hour plus study materials, test fees, a medical exam, etc. Some do it for less if they finish right at 40 hours, but that's very, very rare. Some pay as high as 18 to 20,000 if you're in a high-cost area or you need more hours, but that 12 to $18,000 is a good estimate for the initial license. Instrument rating approximately $8 to $12,000. This includes your flight time in the plane and/or simulator instructor time and exam fees. It can be on the higher side if done in an expensive aircraft or if more hours are done in the airplane versus the simulator. Often done in the same type of plane as private training. commercial pilot's license, single engine, perhaps 15,000 to 30,000. That's a huge broad range. Why? Well, because a big part of the cost is accumulating flight hours between private and commercial stages. Some people might rent a plane and fly a bunch across countries, which costs money for fuel and aircraft rental. Others might defay costs by sharing time with another pilot. There's a method called time building with a safety pilot where two instrument rated pilots take turns flying and logging time. The actual commercial training, learning commercial maneuvers, etc., isn't super expensive. It's the hour building that adds up. If your child does an integrated program from private to commercial continuously, this cost is going to be bundled in, but ultimately it covers reaching that $250 hour mark. Multi-engine rating about $300 to $5,000. Multi-engine planes cost more per hour, maybe about $300 to sometimes $500 per hour, and typically the rating might be about 10 to 15 hours of training. Not everyone does this immediately after commercial single engine, but if the goal is airlines, they will need it sooner or later. Flight instructor certificate, roughly $5 to $10,000 for your initial CFI exam and a few thousand for each additional instructor rating for CFI and MEI. Sometimes, however, a new commercial pilot gets hired into an instructor internship where the school might offset some of his cost in exchange for a work commitment. So adding all that up, to go from zero to commercial pilot with instructor ratings, you might invest on the order of $50 to $80,000 over the course of one to three years. If a college degree is involved, that's separate. Though some aviation universities roll flight training costs into tuition or fees, it's a huge number. I know it's comparable to paying for a 4-year college education in the US, and many people do both college and flight training, which is an enormous financial undertaking. However, remember this is also an investment in a career, one that at the top levels can be very lucrative. That doesn't make writing the checks any easier, but that's something to keep in mind. Now, a few important points about managing these costs. Pay as you go. Unlike college tuition, you generally don't pay for it all up front. You pay for lessons as you take them, or you might keep an account at the flight school. For example, a student might pay for 5 hours at a time. This means you can spread the expenses out over time. The flip side is it requires some discipline to keep at it and not pause training for too long. Pauses can cost more in the end because of skill atrophy. Some schools offer package deals or flat rate courses. Be cautious and understand what's included. And there's one huge red flag. Don't pay everything upfront to a flight school that could go belly up. You might not get that money back. Financing options. There are loans specifically for flight training. Some large flightmies work with lenders to offer student loans, often requiring a co-signer if the trainee is that young. Interest rates can vary. It's something to research and compare. Think of it like paying for trade school. You might take a loan and then have manageable payments once you're working. Scholarships and grants. There are quite a number of aviation scholarships out there. They may not cover the whole cost, but every bit helps. Organizations like AOPA, EAA, Women in Aviation, the 99s for female aviators, OBAP, which is the organization of black aerospace professionals, and many local aviation clubs, offer scholarships ranging from a few hundred to several thousand. Encourage your child to apply. It often requires writing an essay, getting a reference, etc., which is great experience in itself. I've seen students get a few different small scholarships that together paid for their entire instrument rating. Some airlines have started offering cadet programs or tuition reimbursements. For instance, a regional airline might say if you instructed XYZ Academy, our partner, then come work for us, we'll reimburse you up to 10,000 of training costs or offer different bonuses. The landscape here is always changing, so keep an ear out for such opportunities. Military route. If your child is interested in a military career, that can be a way to have the government pay for your flight training. But of course, that's a very, very different style of commitment and lifestyle. It's not and it's very competitive. It's not a decision to make purely for financial reasons, but it's worth noting as an alternative path. Part-time jobs. Many kids and young adults find a chunk of their training by working. I fueled planes at the local airport. It not only earned me some flight time money, but it got me immersed in aviation world and led to a lot of networking opportunities. Other common jobs are anything flexible that can accommodate flight schedules like waiting tables, retail, Uber, etc. If your kid chips in and even a small portion, they will value their training more. As the parent, you don't have to shoulder it all if it would strain your finances. Having them work for it is not a bad thing. Budgeting and planning. It helps to sit down and budget the first stage clearly. For example, how to get through private pilot. Maybe it's 12,000. Where will that come from? Savings, extra work, family contributions, etc. Once they have a private pilot license, there's a natural pause where many people regroup financially before proceeding to advanced training. Some even take a break to finish college or save up. There's no rule that it must be done continuously. Though continuous is ideal if you can manage it. Just be aware that if you stop for years, they'll have to do refresher training to get back up to speed. To be frank, as a flight instructor, I saw students run out of money mid-training all the time, and it's very tough because I often intend to come back, but life can get in the way. So helping your child honestly assess their financial picture before and during training is a big part of the parent role. If you're committing family funds, treat it as you would any large investment. Set expectations. For example, we can fund this much and we expect you to study hard and not waste lessons. At the same time, try not to put so much pressure that they feel they can't take an extra lesson or two if needed. It's a balance. Finally, keep the endgame in mind. Once they land that first airline job or other pilot job, they're earning potential clims drastically. Regional airline first officers might start around $90,000 a year these days. And within a few years, upgrading to captain or moving to a major can significantly increase that. Captains at a regional are going to be making well into the six figures and major airline pilots are going to make even more than that. So unlike some fields, there is a reasonably high income ceiling that can make the training pay off. I often frame it as, would you pay, say, $70,000 for a degree that leads to a $100,000 job? Many people would. If you're watching this on YouTube, you'll realize that I'm in a completely different set of clothes and it's a different day. It got too dark to continue filming. If you're listening to this on Spotify, you probably didn't even notice a difference. So, all right, let's get back where we left off. In summary, yes, it's expensive, but with smart planning, use of available resources, and an understanding that it's an investment in a career, it can be manageable. Don't be afraid to talk openly about money with your aspiring pilot. I actually encourage students to know the value of each lesson and hopefully that inspires them to come prepared so they don't waste a dime of that precious flight time. Your guidance and financial matters. Your guidance and financial matters might be just as important as your encouragement in the flying itself. Safety and maintenance. It's completely natural for parents to worry about safety. Flying has a bit of a Hollywood aura of danger. And you might be thinking, "Is it really safe for my kid to go up in a little airplane?" Let's address that head-on. Safety is the cornerstone of all flight training. Reputable flight schools and instructors prioritize safety above everything else, above scheduling, above costs, above convenience. In practical terms, what does this mean? For one, training aircraft are maintained under very strict regulations. Planes used for hire, which include flight training rentals, must undergo thorough inspections every 100 hours of flight. as well as an annual inspection every year. That's like taking your car for a tuneup every 3,000 mi. Even more detailed than that, certified aircraft mechanics go through the plane regularly to check the engine, the airframe, controls, instruments, and so on. If anything is not up to standard, the plane is taken out of service until it's fixed. As a parent, you can actually ask the flight school, "How do you maintain your aircraft? Can I see the maintenance records?" They should confidently answer that and not be secretive. A school that is hesitant to discuss their maintenance practices or show logs on request would worry me. The good outfits are proud of their well-maintained fleet. Next, the training itself is conducted step by step to ensure safety. In the very beginning, the instructor handles all takeoffs and landings until the student is ready. Even when the student is flying, the instructor can override decisions. There are always two sets of controls in training aircraft. If the student does something wrong, the instructor can instantly take controls to correct it. And believe me, they do. Instructors have lightning fast reflexes when needed. We also drill emergency procedures. Oh, you lost your engine. What do you do? Over and over so that in the unlikely event that something happens, the students and instructors are very well prepared. Weather. Your child will not be flying in dangerous weather. In fact, a big part of flight training is learning to respect weather and make good decisions. If it's very windy or stormy or visibility is poor, the lesson will be postponed or cancelled. Early on, students only fly in pretty much ideal conditions. Daytime, clear skies, light clouds, gentle winds, and then as they advance, they'll do a bit of controlled exposure. Maybe some nighttime flying, maybe learning on a breezy day, but always well within the safe limits and always with the instructor until they're ready to handle it alone. When a student is released to fly solo, the instructor gives them very specific limits. Stay in the local area, no more than 10 miles out, winds below x amount of knots, and no night flying. It's a very supervised independence. Instructor oversight. Even after students start soloing, every solo flight is authorized. I used to physically check the weather and sign off on my students log book, "All right, okay, you can solo today." before they could take the plane keys. It's like a parent signing a permission slip. There's constant monitoring of their progress and ensuring they're only doing what they're trained and ready to do. Now, you might have heard statistics or talk comparing flying to driving. It's true that general aviation, small planes, flying has a higher accident rate per hour than airline flying or driving. But those stats lump together all sorts of pilots. In training, under professional supervision, the risk is significantly mitigated. Most accidents in small planes involve factors like bad weather or fuel mismanagement. Things that student pilots under good instruction won't be doing on their own. I often reassure parents, I'm probably more concerned about your teenager's drive to the airport on the highway than the flight lesson itself. And I mean it. New drivers are at a far greater statistical risk than supervised new pilots. One area you might worry about is what if the engine fails. It's extremely extremely rare, especially with how rigorous trainers are maintained, but we do train for it. From day one, instructors teach students where to land safely if something goes wrong. We always have an emergency landing field in mind. Modern training airplanes can glide surprisingly well without power. Again, that scenario is very unlikely, but I bring it up to emphasize that nothing is left unressed. We plan for even the remotest contingencies. Maintenance quality goes handinhand with safety. I mentioned asking about maintenance. Also note the condition of the planes when you visit. They might be a bit old. Cessna 172s from the 1970s are still flying trainers today, but age isn't a problem if maintained well. It's like having an old classic car that's been kept in top shape. What you don't want is a plane with obvious neglect, like many things broken or inoperative stickers all over it. A squawk or an issue here or there is normal. But if you consistently see aircraft with a lot of non-working equipment, that indicates a culture that tolerates cutting corners. As an instructor, I personally would not teach in an airplane that I felt was unsafe or not maintained properly. Good schools take pride in their fleet safety record. Another safety aspect, medical fitness. Pilots, including student pilots, must pass a basic medical exam. For a young person, this is usually straightforward. An aviation medical examiner checks things like vision, hearing, overall health, and any conditions that could suddenly incapacitate them. This ensures your child is physically up to the task of flying. If there are any medical concerns, for example, they have a condition or on certain medication, definitely bring that up early because it's better to know in advance if any accommodations or special issuance is needed for their medical certificate. Finally, let's talk mindset. Part of becoming a good pilot is learning a safety mindset. a bit of healthy caution, a respect for the limit. Instructors instill this from day one. We have a saying, there's old pilots and bold pilots, but there's no old bold pilots. The idea is that being methodical and cautious is the way to having a long, safe flying career. You'll hear your child start paring things like, "I'm going through my pre-flight checklist," or, "I didn't fly yesterday because the winds were above my limit." That's when you know it's sinking in. Encourage that. Praise their good judgment. If they decide to delay a flight due to safety, that's a sign of maturity. Bottom line, the goal of flight training is not to expose your child to undo risk. It's to teach them how to manage risk responsibly. Every certified flight instructor out there is not only teaching stick and rudder skills, but also acting as a safety coach. And ultimately, the safety record of flight training, especially in structured programs, is very strong. Your child will be operating under a framework of rules and best practices developed over decades of aviation experience. If you ever have a concern, say your child mentioned something that didn't sound right, have a conversation with them or even the instructor with them. Good communication helps everyone stay safe. I won't say there's zero risk. Millions of flight hours are flown by student pilots every year with instructors by their side. Building the next generation of safe pilots. Your child's dream is achievable and can be pursued in a safe manner. We'll make sure of it. Career pathways. Right? Your kid wants to be a pilot, but what kind of pilot might they become? There are more career pathways in aviation than you might realize. Let's explore a few so you know what the end goal might look like and it's okay if your child hasn't figured this out yet. Many student pilots just know they love flying and figure out the specific career later on. Airline pilot, commercial airline. This is definitely the most popular image. Your son or daughter eventually flying big airliners full of passengers wearing the uniform with the captain stripes. It's a great goal. Airline pilots typically start at regional airlines, smaller jets, shorter routes, and then progress to major airlines like Delta or United. The lifestyle involves a lot of travel. They might be away for a few days at a time, but also offers travel benefits and a very good income at the senior levels. If your child is talking about flying for the airlines, they're envisioning this path. The major airline pilot shortage in the recent years means that opportunities over the long term are going to be in abundance. and in the coming decades as many senior pilots retire. So, it's actually a promising time to aim for this. Cargo pilots, similar to airlines, but carrying freight instead of passengers. Think FedEx, UPS, DHL, or other cargo companies. They fly big planes, too. FedEx and UPS pilots fly everything from 767s to MD11s and 747s. Schedules can be different, often overnight flights because packages don't mind redeye flights. The pay and career progression are very much on par with passenger airlines. In many cases, some pilots prefer it. No passengers to deal with, a bit less public spotlight. Next, corporate or business aviation. There are the pilots who fly private jets or business jets for corporations or high netw worth individuals. For example, a large company might have two jets in a flight department to carry their executives around. Or an owner of a private jet hires a pilot to fly their family on trips. These jobs can be very well- paid and often have a lot of variety in destinations. The work schedule might be less predictable on call travel needs, though some have more routine. Example, like a fractional ownership company like NetJets, where pilots have a set schedule. Corporate pilots often enjoy nicer airports and staying in upscale places when they travel, but they also may do more than just fly. They often arrange handling, flight arranging, catering, etc. Especially in a small flight department and flight instructor. Some pilots find they love teaching and they stick with it for the long term. They might become a senior instructor, run a flight school, or become a professor of aviation at a university. While many use instructing as a stepping stone, it can be a fulfilling career on its own. You're home every night. You get to introduce people to aviation, and there's a real pride in shaping new pilots. In the current climate, being a career instructor is actually very valued. With the long-term pilot shortage, we need good instructors. And some are making better money than before due to demand. Military pilot. If your child is interested in serving, becoming a military aviator is another route. They'd have to qualify for military training, which is very competitive, and then commit a number of years to service. The military will give them top-notch training, maybe in fast jets, helicopter, transports, etc., depending on their path. After service, many transition to airlines or other civilian jobs. It's a very different lifestyle and process and doesn't cost money out of pocket. The military pays them to train. It also usually requires at least a college degree and meeting stricter medical and age criteria. It's not for everyone, but I mention it because some parents wonder, should we try for the Air Force? It's one option with its own pros and cons. Other commercial aviation, there are a lot of smaller commercial operations. charter flights, air taxis, bush flying, aerial tourism, firefighting, medical evacuation, agriculture spraying, crop dusters, etc. For example, in Alaska, many pilots fly small planes on supply runs or take fishermen to remote spots. In coastal cities, there are sea plane pilots. There are pilots who fly banners over beaches, tow gliders, or do aerial photography flights. Some of these can be stepping stones to larger jobs or a fun career in themselves. They often require the same commercial pilot qualifications, just a different kind of flying. These jobs might appeal if your child likes variety and maybe a bit of adventure beyond the structured airline world. Now, how to get there? If your child's ultimate goal is airlines, as it is for many, the common pathway we described, training, then instruct, then regional airline, then major airline is the way. If they lean towards corporate, they might aim to get on with a charter company or corporate flight department after some experience. Sometimes regionals first, then corporate, or directly corporate after instructing. Networking is the key here. For cargo, it's similar to airlines. Some start at regionals, then jump to cargo airlines. Others might get on with smaller cargo ops, like flying FedEx feeder routes in a turbo prop, and then move up. One question you might have is, what about college? Should they get a degree or just focus on flying? This is a very important discussion and one that changes very frequently. As of now, major airlines in the US have dropped the requirement for a 4-year degree. It used to be a official requirement for the big carriers. Now, it's not strictly required. However, many pilots still get one, and it can be useful for career flexibility in life in general. If your child can manage doing both college and flight training concurrently or getting a degree after they get their ratings, that's ideal. Some go to universities that have aviation programs that can streamline the process. Earn a bachelor's in aviation, get your licenses and ratings during the program. The downsides are cost. Aviation degrees are very expensive, and putting all of your eggs in one basket. What if they lose their medical at 35 and have no other degree or skill? That's why a degree or backup is nice. Another approach, get a degree in something completely different, engineering, business, anything, while flying on the side, so they have a fallback but still build flight time. There's also the consideration that college grads can get a restricted ATP at 1,000 hours if from an approved aviation program or 1250 with some aviation programs instead of the full 1500 hours. A slight shortcut to the airline for those who go through certain schools. I'd say involve your child in weighing in these options. At 16 or 17, they might be all about flying and be thinking college, you don't need that. But as a parent, you know the value of education. Maybe compromise. Encourage college, but perhaps at a place they can also fly or as an online program while instructing. There are flexible paths. If finances are a concern, sometimes going the professional pilot route quickly and then using their income to fund finishing a degree later works, too. That's personally what I plan on doing. There's no single formula, but do consider long-term resilience. Injury or medical issues can end a flying career, and having a degree or other skill can be a safety net. Lastly, I want to point out an intangible. Being a pilot, whether airline or any pathway, is more than just a job. It becomes a big part of one's identity and community. Your child will be joining a fraternity or sorority of aviators. Essentially, they'll have opportunities to travel, to continuously learn new aircraft, new technologies, and maybe to move into related fields later. Training, management, aviation, business if they want. It's a dynamic industry. I encourage you to learn about the different paths together with your kid. If possible, attend an aviation career seminar or a local air show. Talking to various pilots can open eyes to, I didn't even know that job existed. For instance, I know of somebody who became an air ambulance pilot flying a helicopter. He loved it because he felt like a lifesaver and he's home every night in a small town. Another story is a girl found her calling flying water bombers, firefighting wildfires each summer, then doing charter flying in the offse. There are many fulfilling ways to be a pilot. The good news is whether they choose airlines, corporate cargo, or any other path, the foundational training is the same at first. They don't have to decide right now. The key now is to get started, get the exposure, and they can refine their goals as they progress. And you, as a parent, will likely learn a lot about the world along the way, which means you'll be better equipped to guide them when opportunities arise. How parents can support their child. Your kid might be the one up in the air, but your role on the ground is so incredibly important. I've seen firsthand as an instructor how a parent support or lack thereof can make or break a student's success in flight training. So, let's talk about how you can support your aspiring pilot. Be supportive and take it seriously. I know this sounds basic, but it matters. Show interest in their flying. Ask them to explain what they learned after a lesson. Not only will that help reinforce their learning, you'll also signal that you care about their progress. Even if you don't share their aviation enthusiasm, acknowledge that this is a positive, constructive passion they're pursuing. A teenager learning to fly isn't just learning to fly. They're learning discipline, responsibility, and focus. That's something to be proud of. Celebrate their milestones with them. Their first solo flight, maybe a special dinner, even just a heartfelt, "Wow, I'm so proud of you," goes a long way. Your enthusiasm will boost their confidence. Help them find a mentor or community. Perhaps you know someone who's a pilot, or maybe there's a local pilots association or an experimental aircraft association chapter nearby. Connecting your child with experienced pilots is golden. A mentor can provide advice, answer dumb questions they might be afraid to ask others, and open doors to opportunities. I've seen mentors help students get rides in different aircraft, find scholarships, or get introductions for jobs later. Even if you don't personally know a pilot, consider reaching out to the local airport. Many have flying clubs or just regular people hanging around who would love to talk to young people interested in aviation. The aviation community is generally very welcoming to newcomers. Get involved in the flight school selection and monitor their training environment. This doesn't mean micromanage, but do be an informed consumer. When choosing a flight school or instructor, ask it questions alongside your child. For example, is there a set syllabus or curriculum? How are instructors assigned? Will my kid have one primary instructor? What's the typical time and cost to get a private pilot's license here? Can we meet the chief flight instructor and tour the facility? Your involvement shows the school and instructor that there's a supportive parent in the loop, which is usually welcome. Once training is underway, occasionally check in. Do you feel good with your instructor? Do you feel safe? Are you comfortable with how things are progressing? If something concerns you, like if your child says, "We keep canceling lessons because the plane is always down for maintenance, or instructor so- and so often just doesn't show up or they show up late, you can help address it." Basically, be their advocate if needed, especially if they're young or shy about speaking up. It's similar to being involved in their education at school. You don't do the homework for them, but you keep an eye on whether the school is doing its job. Understand the commitment and help balance it. Learning to fly is timeconuming and can be mentally draining at times. If your child is juggling high school or college and flight training, they'll need good time management. You can support by helping them create a realistic schedule. Maybe Saturday mornings are for flying, Sunday afternoons are for study, weekdays are for school. If they're approaching a big test like the FA written or the check ride, they might need extra study time. Perhaps excuse them from some chores temporarily or help quiz them with flashcards. Also, ensure they're taking care of their health. Proper sleep and nutrition are essential. Nobody does well flying a lesson after pulling an all night or studying for something else. As a parent, simply remind them to get a good night's rest before your lesson. Or making them a hearty breakfast can be a form of support. It shows you know this is a serious endeavor that deserves preparation, financial guidance, and involvement. We discussed costs already you can support by helping manage the money side smartly. This might be as simple as helping them track their flight training expenses in a spreadsheet so everyone is aware of where the money is going and how quickly if you are funding it and how quickly. If you are funding it, involve them in the budget. For instance, we've got X amount of dollars for your private training. Let's see how we're doing after each month. This isn't to stress them, but to teach financial accountability. On the flip side, if they are contributing or taking loans, you can help them understand interest rates or terms, cosign if you need to, etc. I've seen family arrangements like matching programs. We'll pay half if you pay half, which this can motivate the students to work part-time or apply for scholarships to cover their share. Do what fits your family, but keep communication open about money so there are no nasty surprises. Emotional support. Be the cheerleader and the shoulder. Flight training, like any training, has its ups and downs. One week they're elated. They nailed all their landings. Woohoo. The next week they might be defeated. Perhaps they had a scare or struggled with a new concept like radio communication. Rough patches are where parents can make a huge difference. Encourage them when they doubt themselves. A common scenario, a student hits a plateau, maybe struggling to consistently land well, and starts feeling like maybe I'm not cut out for this. This is when a parent saying, "Hey, you're doing something super hard and it's normal to struggle. I believe in you and your instructor does too. Stick with it." This can keep them going. Remind them of how far they've come. For example, 2 months ago, you had never even been in a small plane and now you're talking about complex maneuvers. That's progress. Help them keep perspective. The statistic is often quoted that 80% of student pilots quit before getting their license. And a lot of that is due to frustration or feeling unsupported. With you in their corner, your child will have a much better chance of being in the successful 20%. Set boundaries and contingencies. Support doesn't mean blind indulgence. It's okay, even good, to set expectations. For example, you might say, "We're okay with you taking one afternoon a week to go to the airport, but your grades in school can't slip." Or, "We will fund you through your first solo, but you need to be giving it your best effort and studying hard. If we sense you're not taking it seriously, we'll need to pause." It's fair to have these adult conversations because flying is an adult responsibility. I've had to tell a student who was slacking, I think we should slow down until you're ready to commit. Sometimes that push needs to come from the parent paying the bill. Conversely, if they're overcommitting, like neglecting everything else, you might need to help them find balance. It's all part of guiding them. Join in the experience if you want. Some parents actually get involved by taking an introductory flight themselves or flying along on a lesson or two with the instructor's permission. This is sometimes possible on like cross-country flights. If you're open to it, experiencing a flight from the passenger seat of the small plane can be very eye opening. You'll see what it's like up there and probably gain appreciation for what they're learning. At minimum, visit the airport with them occasionally. Watch their takeoffs and landings if you can. It's kind of like going to their soccer games. Being there, even on the sidelines, means a lot. In summary, being the parent of a student pilot might involve being a bit of a coach, cheerleader, manager, and safety net all at once. You don't have to know anything about flying to be effective in these roles. Just keep the communication open. Ask them to explain things to you, even if you secretly find it all like it's Greek. It helps them learn when they teach you. Keep an eye on their well-being, mental and physical, as they train. And remind them of the dream they're chasing. There might be days they lose sight of the long-term goal and you can gently refocus them. Remember how much you love flying. It's tough now, but imagine when you're taking us on vacation flight someday as the pilot. I still remember my mom saying when I was buried in books studying for the exam, "I can't wait for your flight benefits one day." Actually, she still tells me that to this day. That simple sentence lit, it's a fire in you to push through the hard stuff. You're not just allowing this journey. You're a part of it. And one day when your child is a licensed pilot, they're going to remember that you were a crucial part of their flight training. Let's tackle some common myths. The world of aviation comes with its fair share of myths and misconceptions. Let's clear up some of the common ones that often concern parents and students. You might have heard some of these or just have these assumptions. It's important to separate the myth from the reality. Myth: You need perfect vision to be a pilot. Reality generally do not need 2020 eyesight. Glasses or contacts are acceptable. Pilots just need their vision to be corrected to 2020 or 2040 depending on the class of medical. 2040 for private, 2020 for airline pilots. Many airline pilots wear glasses. Only extreme vision problems that can't be corrected would disqualify someone. So, if your child wears glasses, that's usually not an issue. They'll just have a limitation on their medical. Saying something like they must wear corrective lenses when flying, just like on a driver's license. Myth: Pilots have to be math geniuses or physics wizards. Reality. While it's true that aviation involves some math and science, it's mostly very basic arithmetic and basic physics concepts that you are taught during training. You don't need to be a calculus expert or have an advanced physics degree. For example, they'll use math to calculate fuel burn distances or weight and balance. But this is often plugging numbers into simple formulas, and many even use electronic flight computers nowadays. If your child can handle high school algebra and read graphs, they can handle the academics of flight training. It does require studying and understanding new concepts like how lift works or how to interpret weather charts, but it's a very learnable skill. I've taught students from all sorts of education backgrounds. The successful ones are not those who aced AP physics, but those who are determined, disciplined, and willing to study. So, don't let I'm not a math person scare anyone off from flying. Myth: Flying is dangerous, especially for a young, inexperienced person. Reality. We addressed safety in depth earlier, but to reiterate, flight training is as safe as we can possibly make it. It's true that there is an inherent risk in aviation, just as there is in driving or in sports, etc., but your child will be operating under the guidance of professionals and within strict safety margins. When done responsibly, flying a small plane is a very calculated activity, not a reckless daredevil stunt. Statistically, general aviation has more risk than airline flying. But during training, the risks are mitigated by instructor supervision and a phased approach to solo flying. Think of it this way. Thousands of students solo and get their pilot's license each year uneventfully. The incidents that do happen often come from people venturing outside their training or pushing their limits, which a student under instructor oversight simply won't be doing. As a parent, the best way to ensure safety is exactly what you're doing. Learning about it, choosing a good school, and encouraging good habits. So, yes, there's risk, but it's managed risk. We don't let fear control us. We respect it and use it to stay sharp and safe. Myth: You have to be rich to become a pilot. Reality. It's true that flight training is expensive. We talk numbers already, but you don't have to be a millionaire or have a trust fund. Plenty of pilots come from middle class or even very humble backgrounds. There are lots of ways to make it work financially. Savings, loans, working during training, scholarships, etc. Many families budget for it like they would for college. Additionally, there are aviation programs in some high schools or community colleges that can lower cost in military or cadet pathways that can offset cost. So, while money is a hurdle, it's not an impenetrable barrier for those who are resourceful and determined. With the current demand for pilots, there are even airlines and companies offering tuition reimbursement. Bottom line, if your child has the talent and drive, there are ways to overcome the financial challenge. It shouldn't be dismissed as only for the rich. It might require sacrifice, but it's an investment that can pay off big time. Pilots need a college degree or military experience to get hired. Reality: This used to be more true in the past, but times have drastically changed. Civilian trained pilots, meaning no military background, are now the majority of airline new hires. It's absolutely possible to go from civilian flight training to airline pilot without military service. As for college, many major airlines have dropped the requirement of a 4-year degree. It can still be a plus, and it's often encouraged, but it's not officially mandatory. Regional airlines do not require a degree at all. So, if your child isn't interested in college, they can still pursue this career. Though, as we discussed, having a degree is a good backup plan. There are also two-year degrees in aviation that incorporate flight training, which is another option. In short, neither the military nor a university diploma is a must for becoming a pilot. Those are just two among many pathways. Myth: Piloting is an unstable career. Airlines furlow pilots all the time. Or what if automation replaces pilots? Reality. The aviation industry like any has cycles. It's true that after 9/11 or during the 2020 pandemic, airlines furled meaning temporarily laid off pilots. However, historically, pilots have been in demand, and the career tends to be stable over the long term, especially if you make it to a major airline. Right now, over the long term, there's a well-known pilot shortage as baby boomer pilots retire at 65 and too few trained in the last decade to replace them. This shortage is projected to continue for many years, which means that high demand, good job security, and rising salaries. As for automation, yes, autopilots are very advanced, but airplanes absolutely still need human pilots and will for the foreseeable future. Every time a tech bro says planes will fly themselves, a new real world situation comes up that chills the need for human decision-making. Be it handling an emergency or just the complexities of weather and air traffic. Drones and remote operations are growing in niche areas, but nobody is talking about single pilot or pilotless airliners in any serious way for the next few decades. And even if it happens, it'll start with cargo. So, I wouldn't worry about your child's career becoming obsolete overnight. Myth: Becoming a pilot means you're never home and can't have a normal family life. Reality: Pilots travel as part of their job. Yes, but it's not like they live out of a suitcase 365 days a year. Schedules vary. A junior airline pilot might go do 3 to 4 day trips and then have a few days off, while a senior pilot might choose schedules that get them home every night, like flying out and back. Many airlines also have options like commuting, living in one city and flying out of another, which can be very tough but manageable with planning, or bid lines that can be optimized for more time off in a block. I know plenty of pilots who make all their kids important events. They just bid their schedules around them. It might mean missing some birthdays or holidays early in their career, but they often can swap or trade to make things work. Additionally, not all pilot jobs are airlines. A corporate pilot might be home most of the week and then only fly when needed. A flight instructor is typically home every night. So, yes, this job requires understanding from the family about travel and odd hours, but it's not true that pilots can't have families. In fact, many airlines are improving quality of life and offering more predictable schedules because they know it helps retain pilots. So, if that's a concern, will my kid ever settle down if they're a pilot? Rest assured, it's quite possible to have a very normal family life just with a somewhat non-traditional work schedule. Those are some of the big ones. If you and your child encounter other claims like pilots have to retire by 50, not true at 65 and by law on the airlines and you and you can fly general aviation as long as you're healthy or only men can be pilots. Very not true. Women are pilots too and actively encouraged today or pilots make no money starting out. Historically, that is true, but it's improved a lot in the last few years. Feel free to fact check them with credible sources or ask someone in the industry. There are a lot of old tales out there. So, always consider the source. The bottom line is don't let myths scare your child or you away. If this is their passion, the path to becoming a pilot is more accessible and inclusive today than ever. And you're doing exactly the right thing by educating yourself. That way, when someone at a dinner party says, "Oh, I heard small planes just drop out of the sky." you can confidently respond with the facts and perhaps a bit of an eye roll and correct them. Red flags when choosing a flight school. Choosing a flight school or training program is a big deal. Not all flight schools are created equal. Some are fantastic and will set your child up for success. Others, well, let's just say I've heard some horror stories of wasted money and very bad experiences. As a parent, you can bring your consumer savvy to the table and help spot any red flags. Here are some things to watch out for. Number one, pricing information is unclear or not provided upfront. If a school's website or staff won't give you a straight answer about lesson rates, aircraft rentals, costs, or estimated totals for a license, be cautious. Training is expensive, yes, but it shouldn't be a mystery. A reputable school will typically have a rate sheet and even a rough course estimate. If they dance around the topic and say, "We'll figure that out as we go." That's a huge red flag. They might be hiding the true cost or plan to nickel and dime you later. Transparency is key. you deserve to know what you're getting into financially. Number two, mandatory large upfront payments for enrollment. Some flight schools offer package deals where you prepay a chunk or even the whole training for a slight discount. That's not inherently bad, but be very wary of any school that requires a huge sum up front with no proven track record. Sadly, there have been cases of students paying $50,000 upfront to an academy that then shuts down, leaving them with no refund. If a school insists you must pay in advance for 50 hours or your whole private pilot cost before you even start, I'd consider that a red flag. At the very least, research the heck out of the school's reputation and see if they have refund policies. In general, pay as you go or small increments are safer. Never feel pressured to fork over all the money at once. Once they have your money, their incentive to keep you happy can diminish. Number three, unreasonable cancellation or refund policies. Ask them about their cancellation policy for lessons. Most places have something like if you cancel within 24 hours, you might be charged an hour of the instructor's time. That's to prevent no shows, and that's fine. That's pretty standard. But if you see something crazy like any canceled lesson incurs a $300 fee, or they charge you for weather cancellations or maintenance cancellations that aren't the student's fault, that's a big red flag. Or if they make refunds nearly impossible, like if you decide to leave, they won't refund any used money in your account. You want fairness. A policy that's overly punitive or doesn't allow for unavoidable issues is not studentfriendly. Number four, odd or overly restrictive contract clauses. This is a very niche one, but read any training agreement carefully. There have been some absurd clauses out there like finding students for minor infractions or forbidding them from training elsewhere. One story that came up was a school that had a clause prohibiting students from taking pictures on the ramp and threatened fin. That kind of weird secrecy or legal intimidation is a huge red flag. Another example is the contract says no refunds under any circumstances or we guarantee you a job if you sign this but with lots of strings attached. Approach with caution. Legit schools don't need bizarre clauses. They operate on trust in good service. Number five, lack of structured syllabus or training plan. When you talk to the school or instructor, ask them, "Do you follow a syllabus or curriculum for training?" They should say yes and ideally show it to you. Many use FA or commercially published syllabi. If they hem and haw like, oh, we just tailor it and as we go, no fix syllabus, that can lead to disorganization. Each instructor doing their own thing without a plan can mean gaps in training or inefficiency. Consistency is key. This doesn't mean training can't be flexible, but there should be goals and lessons outlined. A red flag is if each lesson seems random or the instructor isn't recording progress in some way. You want to see that they have a sense of phase. Number one, pre-olo training. Number two, solo, cross country, etc. Many schools even give students a training record binder or online portal to track progress. High turnover or disinterested instructors. It's true that flight instructing is often a transient job. Instructors move on to the airlines, but you can get a feel if an instructor has five students and can't remember your kid's name by lesson three, or they seem to be in a rush always. Not good. Also, if a school has burned through four chief instructors in a year, something's up. During your visit, maybe ask the instructors how long they've been there. A place that churns through instructors might have management or pay issues. Additionally, consistency is important. Your child should primarily fly with one instructor or a set small team. If a school switches the student to whoever is free each time, random instructor assignment assignment every lesson, that's not ideal and can hinder learning. It's a sign they may value squeezing every dollar keeping planes flying non-stop over tailored teaching. Small schools might only have two to three instructors, which is fine. Big ones might have dozens, but usually still assign a primary CFI to each student. Poor condition of aircraft or secretive maintenance practices. We touched on this in safety, but as a red flag, if you tour the facility and see planes with obvious issues not being addressed or the place just looks like a junkyard, run away. Also ask, "What's your policy on maintenance squawks? How quickly are they addressed?" If they waffle or say, "Well, we try to defer things as long as possible to keep the cost down." That's bad. If they won't let you see a plane up close, or maintenance logs when asked politely, that's suspicious. You don't need to audit them, but a good school will be transparent about their emphasis on maintenance. Clean, well-ared for airplanes. They don't have to be new, but they should be obviously airworthy at if every plane you peek at has multiple inoperative stickers on key instruments or, I don't know, duct tape on control yolks, not a good sign. This isn't a used car lot, safety is non-negotiable, overemphasis on sales and marketing speak. If the school's rep or website is all about flashy promises, become an airline pilot guaranteed, and we're the number one pilot factory with the newest fleet, but they won't directly answer your detailed questions. Watch out. A solid school lets results and reputation speak more than marketing. They should be proud, yes, but not making pie in the sky guarantees. Also, if they push you to sign up now, prices will rise. Reserve your spot with a deposit today and you're not ready. That pressure is a mini red flag. Legit programs will welcome you when you're ready and not use high pressure sales tactics. No reviews or bad reviews. Do your homework. In this internet age, take advantage of it. Look up reviews of the flight school or academy. Keep in mind one or two disgruntled voices might not tell the whole story, but look for patterns. If multiple people mention issues with billing or unsafe practices, steer clear. If you can't find anything about them and it's not a brand new school, that's odd, too. Ask on aviation forums or local pilot communities, "What do you think of XYZ Flight School?" Pilots tend to be very honest in their opinions. I've seen people get steered away from sketchy operations by simply asking around. Conversely, if a school has a great reputation, you'll hear that, too. I know that's a lot to consider. Don't be overwhelmed. Once you find a good match, you'll know. The instructors will be welcoming, the planes will be ready, the business side handled professionally, your child will feel comfortable and excited to train there. It's just like choosing a college or even a good driving school. You do a bit of research, trust your gut, and make the best choice you can. And remember, if you start at one place and things change or feel off, it is okay to switch flight schools or instructors. It might cost a little extra time to get a new instructor up to speed, but in the long run, it's worth it to be in the right training environment. I've taken transfer students who came from a bad situation elsewhere. It's like a breath of fresh air for them to realize not everywhere is like that. So keep these red flags in mind, but once you commit, give the school and instructor a chance and reassess if needed after a few months. You've got this. All right, my final advice to you. As we wrap up, I want to speak to you instructor to parent. Watching your child pursue flight training is going to be an adventure for both of you. You'll feel pride, you'll feel anxiety, and you'll feel maybe a lighter wallet, but hopefully you'll also feel joy seeing them move toward a dream. Number one, keep the big picture in mind. There will be day-to-day challenges, a failed written test, a bad weather week where no lessons happened. Maybe an instructor change mid-training, etc. These can be frustrating in the moment. Help your child see them as temporary bumps, not permanent roadblocks. The journey to becoming a pilot is a marathon, not a sprint. Encourage resilience. Sometimes a student might want to quit after a setback. That's when your voice says, "Hey, remember why you started. This is what you've always wanted." Take a breath, maybe a short break, and then let's tackle it again. Final piece of advice number two, enjoy the journey. It's not all serious. There will be fun, lots of fun. Encourage your child to relish the cool moments. The first time they fly an airplane by themselves, the first time they take you or friend up after getting licensed, maybe flying to a pancake breakfast at a rural airport. Those are memories that last a lifetime. You being part of it will make it even sweeter. I have a picture of me and my dad on my phone on the first time that I took him flying after getting my license. I, as a newly minted private pilot, him is my passenger. The smile on his face was just as big as mine. I want you to have a moment like this with your child. It's worth all the studying and dollars in sweaty palms. Number three, network and seek advice. You've done great by watching this video and educating yourself. Don't stop here. The aviation world has a lot of resources for parents of young pilots. There are online communities. Local pilot groups. Even school counselors might know of aviation programs. Plug into those. Other parents who've been through it can offer tips. Oh, XYZ scholarship is great. Apply for that. Or, oh, we call pulled to the airport together. You don't have to do this alone. Number four, trust the process. Once you've done your due diligence, pick a good school, ensured the instructor is a good fit. Try to relax a bit and trust them. It's like sending your kid to driving school or off to college. You equip them, then you let them actually do it. Hovering beyond a point, can impede their growth. Aviation, perhaps more than many pursuits, instills responsibility. You'll likely see your child mature and grow as they advance in training. It's truly impressive. That timid student who was nervous to key the mic and talk to air traffic control will turn into a confident pilot who makes decisions and problem solves in real time. Let them blossom in that. Be there to catch them if they stumble, but also give them room to prove themselves. It's a delicate balance, I know. Lastly, I want to say congratulations. Really, if your child has discovered a passion and is going for it, that's something to celebrate. A lot of young people drift without direction. Yours has pointed at the sky and said that that's where I want to go. How incredible is that? Your support can help them turn this dream into a reality. Pilots often credit someone in their life, an encouraging parent, a supportive spouse, a mentor as a key factor in their success. You have the opportunity to be that person for your son or daughter. It might not always be easy. We've talked about plenty of challenges, but it will be rewarding. One day, maybe a few years from now, you might be sitting on a commercial flight and hear your kid's voice come over the PA. Hello everyone, this is Captain Blank speaking up from the flight deck and you'll remember when they were a kid with a crazy dream. Or maybe you'll just be at their flight school graduation or at the airport when they do a solo cross country flight to visit grandma. Big or small, these moments will make you burst with pride. Thank you for taking the time to arm yourself with knowledge and for being there for your future pilot. If you have any questions that I didn't cover, please reach out and drop a comment. I'm happy to help clarify. After all, it takes a village to bring up a pilot and to make a pilot. It does. All right, blue skies and tailwinds to you and your child's aviation journey. Your support is the wind beneath their wings. As cheesy as that sounds, it's true. Enjoy the ride, and who knows, maybe I'll see you out at the airport someday, proudly watching your kid pre-flight an airplane. Until then, take care, fly safe, and I'll see you next
